[4:2:344,478] areas. Additionally, this Section includes issues  

[4:3:344,488] that are statewide in nature such as lease/revenue  

[4:4:344,498] issues, bonds, and local government issues.

[1:0:134,43] 8120   Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training  

[2:0:66,64] The Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training (POST)   is responsible for raising the competence level of law  

[2:1:66,74] enforcement officers in California by establishing minimum selection and training standards, improving management practices,  

[2:2:66,84] and providing financial assistance to local agencies relating to the training of law enforcement officers. POST promotes training  

[2:3:66,95] to improve quality, integrity, accountability, and cooperation; encourages new ideas; explores and uses appropriate  

[2:4:66,105] technologies; and delivers relevant, client-based programs and services.

[14:0:66,567] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[14:1:65,586] • Increased Peace Officer Training-The Budget includes $14.9 million ongoing General Fund to restore POST to its historical  

[14:2:76,596] budget level prior to the decline in fine and fee revenues. This funding will allow POST to restore many of the program cuts it  

[14:3:76,606] was forced to make in recent years, including the Quality Assurance Program, Instructor Development and Academy  

[14:4:76,616] Instructor Training, and the Robert Presley Institute of Criminal Investigations. It will also be used to implement new, and  

[14:5:76,626] update existing, training curriculum so that courses reflect current best practices. In addition, the Budget includes $20 million  

[14:6:76,637] ongoing General Fund for local law enforcement to meet training standards. For fiscal years 2019-20 and 2020-21, these  

[14:7:76,647] local assistance funds will be prioritized for use of force and de-escalation trainings.

[7:0:66,302] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,321] 6500 - STANDARDS  

[8:0:66,341] The Standards Program establishes job-related selection and training standards for peace officers and dispatchers. It also  

[8:1:66,351] provides management consultation to local law enforcement agencies. Activities include development of examinations and  

[8:2:66,361] counseling local law enforcement agencies on ways to improve management practices.  

[9:0:66,382] The Program conducts applied research in the areas of peace officer selection and training, operational procedures and  

[9:1:66,392] program evaluation to meet statutory requirements and to provide management guidance to local law enforcement agencies. It  

[9:2:66,402] also facilitates the development and implementation of new programs for local agencies by serving as a clearinghouse of  

[9:3:66,412] successful program information. Finally, it conducts accreditation and peace officer feasibility studies.  

[9:4:66,430] 6505 - TRAINING  

[10:0:66,448] The Training Program increases the competence of law enforcement personnel by developing and certifying courses that meet  

[10:1:66,458] identified training needs. This activity requires scheduling and quality control of such courses, and assisting law enforcement  

[10:2:66,468] agencies in presenting necessary training and career development programs to their officers. The Commission assesses  

[10:3:66,478] training on a continuing basis to ensure that evolving training needs are met. The curricula cover a wide variety of topics  

[10:4:66,489] necessary to satisfy statutory and regulatory mandates, maintain competence in police work, and address the training needs of  

[10:5:66,499] law enforcement agency personnel. Curricula content is updated regularly. The Program also presents advanced training for  

[10:6:66,509] law enforcement supervisors and executives through its Command College and the Supervisory Leadership Institute, and for  

[10:7:66,519] trainers through the Basic Academy Instructor Certificate Program and the Instructor Development Institute. Other specialty  

[10:8:66,529] programs include the Institute of Criminal Investigation.  

[11:0:66,547] Job-related selection and training standards for peace officers and dispatchers, established by the Standards Program, are  

[11:1:66,557] enforced through inspections of local agencies receiving state aid to ensure they are adhering to minimum state standards.  

[11:2:66,575] 6510 - PEACE OFFICER TRAINING  

[12:0:66,595] The Peace Officer Training Program provides financial assistance to participating jurisdictions for instructional costs associated  

[12:1:66,606] with selected training courses. Funding is also provided for the cost of student travel and per diem expenses associated with  

[12:2:66,616] training presentations to enable line officers to receive in-service training in areas of critical need. To encourage and assist local  

[12:3:66,626] law enforcement agencies to meet and maintain minimum standards in the selection and training of law enforcement officers,  

[12:4:66,636] the Commission provides financial assistance to all 58 counties, approximately 346 cities, and numerous specialized districts  

[12:5:66,646] and local agencies which have agreed to meet the Commission's standards.  

[12:6:66,664] 6515 - POST ADMINISTRATION  

[13:0:66,685] The   objective of the California Commission on Peace Officer Standards and Training is to be the leading organization in  

[13:1:66,695] establishing professional standards, assuring top-quality training, and providing effective services to develop and support  

[13:2:66,705] California law enforcement personnel.   California POST utilizes the following eight bureaus to insure uniform standards and  

[13:3:66,715] compliance are applied to over 600 law enforcement agencies and 40 police academies statewide: Basic Training;  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[11:0:66,532] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:1:66,550] 6530 - STATE PUBLIC DEFENDER  

[12:0:66,570] The objective of the Office of the State Public Defender, upon assignment of cases exclusively from the California Supreme  

[12:1:66,581] Court, is to provide legal services in capital appeals to persons who do not have the financial means to employ private counsel.  

[12:2:66,591] The Office of the State Public Defender focuses its resources exclusively on post-conviction proceedings following the  

[12:3:66,601] judgment of death. The State Public Defender is headquartered in Oakland, and has a regional office in Sacramento.

[1:0:222,43] 8260   California Arts Council  

[1:1:66,64] The California Arts Council (Council) consists of eleven members, nine appointed by the Governor and one each appointed by  

[1:2:66,74] the President pro Tempore of the Senate and the Speaker of the Assembly. The Council establishes general policy and  

[1:3:66,84] approves program allocations.  

[2:0:66,105] The Council recognizes that the Arts are essential for the cultural, educational, social and economic development of California.  

[2:1:66,115] The Council seeks to further its mandates and services to the public through the development of partnerships with the public  

[2:2:66,125] and private sectors and by providing support to the state's non-profit arts and cultural community, which are broad-based and  

[2:3:66,135] extended across the state from its largest metropolitan areas to its most rural areas.  

[3:0:66,156] The Council is statutorily required to:  

[3:1:65,174] • Encourage artistic awareness, participation, and expression among the citizens of California.  

[3:2:65,190] • Help independent local groups develop their own arts programs.  

[4:0:65,206] • Promote the employment of artists and those skilled in crafts in both the public and private sectors.  

[4:1:65,222] • Provide for the exhibition of art works in public buildings throughout California.  

[4:2:65,238] • Enlist the aid of all state agencies in the task of ensuring the fullest expression of our artistic potential.

[1:0:154,43] 8385   California Citizens Compensation Commission  

[1:1:66,64] The California Citizens Compensation Commission is responsible for setting the salaries and benefits for the Governor,  

[1:2:66,74] Lieutenant Governor, State Legislators, Attorney General, Secretary of State, Controller, Treasurer, Superintendent of Public  

[1:3:66,84] Instruction, Insurance Commissioner, and Board of Equalization members.  

[2:0:66,105] The seven-member Commission meets annually, no later than June 30, to determine if any changes should be made to the  

[2:1:66,115] salaries and benefits of the state's elected officials. The decisions of the Commission become effective the first Monday in  

[2:2:66,125] December following the annual meeting.  

[3:0:66,146] The members of the Commission are appointed by the Governor. Three members represent the public, two members represent  

[3:1:66,156] the business community, and two members represent labor organizations. Each member serves a six-year term and the terms  

[3:2:66,166] are staggered.  

[4:0:66,186] The Commission's budget provides for travel expenses and stipends for the annual meeting. Department of Human Resources  

[4:1:66,197] staff provides support to the Commission using its existing resources.

[1:0:183,43] 8420   State Compensation Insurance Fund  

[2:0:66,64] State Compensation Insurance Fund (State Fund) is a public enterprise fund established through legislation that was originally  

[2:1:66,74] enacted in 1913. State Fund is governed by a board of directors with authority comparable to that of the governing body of a  

[2:2:66,84] private insurance carrier. State Fund has its own separate budget process that is presented to its board of directors for  

[2:3:66,95] approval.  

[3:0:66,115] State Fund's purpose is to provide fairly priced workers' compensation insurance, make California workplaces safe, restore  

[3:1:66,125] injured workers, and operate as the continuously available market for workers' compensation insurance for employers located  

[3:2:66,135] in California. Pursuant to the Insurance Code, State Fund is to be self-supporting with revenues from premiums on policies  

[3:3:66,146] written and from investment income. State Fund does not receive any financial support from the State of California, and the  

[3:4:66,156] state is not liable for any obligations of State Fund. Almost all of State Fund's employees are hired through the California civil  

[3:5:66,166] service process and are actually employees of the state.  

[4:0:66,186] State Fund is considered a related organization to the State of California because State Fund was created by the state and  

[4:1:66,197] because the Governor appoints a voting majority of State Fund's board of directors. However, the State of California is not  

[4:2:66,207] financially liable or responsible for the operations or solvency of State Fund.  

[5:0:66,227] Most of the state agencies and departments of the State of California are legally uninsured for workers' compensation coverage  

[5:1:66,237] and benefits for injured employees. Separate from its issuance of workers' compensation insurance policies to private  

[5:2:66,248] employers, State Fund has been engaged by the California Department of Human Resources to provide workers'  

[5:3:66,258] compensation claims administration services for these legally uninsured departments, agencies, boards, commissions, and  

[5:4:66,268] other subdivisions of the State of California under a Master Services Agreement. Under this agreement, the state provides a  

[5:5:66,278] deposit out of which State Fund pays compensation benefits to injured state workers and medical benefits to health providers,  

[5:6:66,288] and State Fund is reimbursed by state agencies for benefit amounts paid plus a service fee equal to the cost of these services.  

[6:0:66,309] For informational purposes only, the table further below   provides summary data on actual and estimated workers'  

[6:1:66,319] compensation claims costs paid by state agencies. All workers' compensation costs are included in the individual budgets of  

[6:2:66,329] the responsible state agencies. Costs also include administrative charges under the Master Services Agreement.

[1:0:184,43] 8570   Department of Food and Agriculture  

[2:0:66,64] The California Department of Food and Agriculture serves the citizens of California by promoting and protecting a safe, healthy  

[2:1:66,74] food supply, and enhancing local and global agricultural trade, through efficient management, innovation, and sound science,  

[2:2:66,84] with a commitment to environmental stewardship.  

[3:0:66,105] The goals of the California Department of Food and Agriculture are to:  

[3:1:65,123] • Promote and protect the diverse local and global marketability of the California agricultural brand which represents superior  

[3:2:76,133] quality, value, and safety.  

[4:0:65,149] • Optimize resources through collaboration, innovation, and process improvements.  

[5:0:65,165] • Connect rural and urban communities by supporting and participating in educational programs that emphasize a mutual  

[5:1:76,175] appreciation of the value of diverse food and agricultural production systems.  

[5:2:65,192] • Improve regulatory efficiency through proactive coordination with stakeholders.  

[5:3:65,208] • Invest in employee development and succession planning efforts.  

[6:0:66,225] Because the California Department of Food and Agriculture's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the  

[7:0:66,236] Department has a capital outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see  

[7:1:66,246] "Infrastructure Overview."

[6:0:66,552] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:0:66,570] 6570 - AGRICULTURAL PLANT AND ANIMAL HEALTH; PEST PREVENTION; FOOD SAFETY SERVICES  

[8:0:66,591] The objective of this program is to prevent the introduction and establishment of serious plant and animal pests and diseases to  

[8:1:66,601] California, promote judicious antimicrobial use and stewardship   and protect the safety of California's dairy, eggs and meat  

[8:2:66,611] products exempt from Federal inspection. In particular, the program is focused on pests and diseases that can: (1) be  

[8:3:66,621] transmitted to humans, (2) inflict catastrophic financial loss on California's farmers, ranchers, and associated businesses, (3)  

[8:4:66,631] have severe negative impact on the environment, or (4) adversely affect the supply of agricultural products to the consumer.  

[8:5:66,649] 6575 - MARKETING; COMMODITIES AND AGRICULTURAL SERVICES  

[9:0:66,669] California agriculture produces over 400 different crops, which enter state, national, and international commerce. The  

[9:1:66,680] objectives of this program are to assure orderly domestic and international marketing of safe and quality agricultural  

[9:2:66,690] commodities, promote consumer protection, increase food access, ensure fair pricing practices, oversee industry-supported  

[9:3:66,700] grading services, and maintain standards of measurement which provide a basis of value comparison, fair competition in the  

[9:4:66,710] marketplace, and establish quality standards for conventional and alternative fuels and automotive products.  

[10:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:149,32] 8570   Department of Food and Agriculture - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] This program also provides licenses to eligible California cannabis cultivators and maintains a statewide cannabis tracking  

[3:1:66,79] system.  

[4:0:66,99] Additionally, this program provides support to governmental agencies that work to protect the nation's food supply and the  

[4:1:66,109] environment by monitoring for chemical contaminants.  

[4:2:66,127] 6580 - ASSISTANCE TO FAIRS AND COUNTY AGRICULTURAL ACTIVITIES  

[4:3:66,145] This program provides limited fiscal and policy oversight to the network of California fairs.  

[4:4:66,163] The State has a network of 79 fairs including county fairs, citrus fruit fairs, District Agricultural Associations and the California  

[4:5:66,173] State Fair (an independent state agency). State oversight of these local fairs includes attendance of board meetings and  

[4:6:66,183] periodic financial reviews and audits.  

[4:7:66,201] 6590 - GENERAL AGRICULTURAL ACTIVITIES  

[5:0:66,218] This program provides the fiscal and policy oversight of the federal grants awarded that promote California agriculture, and for  

[5:1:66,229] all CDFA Greenhouse Gas Reduction Program activities which are designed to reduce greenhouse gas emissions in  

[5:2:66,239] agriculture.  

[6:0:66,256] In addition, this program serves as the central point of contact for logistical coordination of all departmental resources, provides  

[6:1:66,267] industry and agency coordination on environmental issues affecting agriculture, and provides centralized communications to  

[6:2:66,277] California's agricultural industry, including County Agricultural Commissioners and the statewide fairgrounds.  

[6:3:66,295] This program also partially reimburses County Agricultural Commissioners' Offices for carrying out agricultural programs  

[6:4:66,305] authorized by the Food and Agricultural Code under the supervision of CDFA.  

[6:5:66,322] 9900 - EXECUTIVE, MANAGEMENT AND ADMINISTRATIVE SERVICES  

[6:6:66,340] Executive and Management Services include the executive leadership of the Secretary's Office. The Secretary's Office sets  

[6:7:66,350] priorities and policies to protect, support, and promote agriculture in the State of California, and helps to protect the health and  

[6:8:66,361] welfare of the public and the environment.  

[7:0:66,378] Administrative Services provides centralized administrative support to the Department through fiscal operations, employee-

[7:1:66,388] employer relations, personnel management, employee development, and general business services.

[9:0:66,588] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[10:0:66,606] The California Department of Food and Agriculture's (CDFA) facilities support its operations serving the citizens of California by  

[10:1:66,617] promoting and protecting a safe and healthy food supply and enhancing local and global agricultural trade through efficient  

[10:2:66,627] management, innovation, and sound science, with a commitment to environmental stewardship. These facilities are in 79  

[10:3:66,637] locations in Arizona, Hawaii, and throughout California. The CDFA rents or owns approximately 713,000 square feet of  

[10:4:66,647] facilities, including 42 field offices, 11 laboratories, 16 border protection stations, 9 employee residences, and a headquarters in  

[10:5:66,657] Sacramento.

[1:0:185,43] 8620   Fair Political Practices Commission  

[2:0:66,64] The Fair Political Practices Commission has primary responsibility for the impartial administration, implementation, and  

[2:1:66,74] enforcement of the Political Reform Act of 1974, as amended by the voters and Legislature. The purpose of the Act is to restore  

[2:2:66,84] confidence in governmental processes. The major objectives of the Commission are to:  

[3:0:65,102] • Provide education about the Act and its requirements to the public and the regulated community including public officials,  

[3:1:76,112] candidates, and lobbyists, and assist with compliance.  

[4:0:65,129] • Ensure that election campaign contribution and expenditure data is fully and accurately disclosed so that the voters may be  

[4:1:76,139] fully informed.  

[5:0:65,155] • Enforce the provisions of the Act and regulations fairly and with due process.  

[5:1:65,171] • Regulate the activities of lobbyists and disclose their finances.  

[6:0:65,187] • Provide for the disclosure of assets and income of public officials, which may affect their official actions, to avoid conflicts of  

[6:1:76,198] interest or appearances of impropriety.

[1:0:208,43] 8640   Political Reform Act of 1974  

[2:0:66,64] This budget identifies the amounts required to be appropriated by the Legislature to the Fair Political Practices Commission to  

[2:1:66,74] carry out its duties under the Act, the amounts to be appropriated to other state agencies to carry out their duties under the Act,  

[2:2:66,84] and, for informational purposes, the continuing appropriation made by the Act to the Commission, adjusted for any cost-of-living  

[2:3:66,95] change.

[1:0:208,43] 8660   Public Utilities Commission  

[2:0:66,64] The California Public Utilities Commission (PUC) regulates critical and essential services such as privately-owned  

[3:0:66,74] telecommunications, electric, natural gas, and water companies, in addition to overseeing railroad/rail transit and transportation  

[3:1:66,84] companies. The PUC is the only agency in the state charged with protecting private utility consumers. As such, the PUC  

[3:2:66,95] regulates services and utilities, protects consumers, safeguards the environment, and assures Californians' access to safe and  

[3:3:66,105] reliable utility infrastructure and services.

[5:0:66,243] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,261] 6680 - REGULATION OF UTILITIES 

[7:0:66,279] Californians spend more than $50 billion annually for services from industries regulated by the PUC. This includes 6 electricity  

[7:1:66,289] utilities, 900 telecommunications carriers, 120 water and sewer utilities, 5 natural gas utilities with approximately 10.8 million  

[7:2:66,299] customers, 4 gas facility owners, and more than 3,155 small mobile home park and propane operators.  

[8:0:66,317] The PUC oversees the safety of electric, communications, natural gas, and propane gas utility systems. Safety functions  

[8:1:66,327] include both backward-looking investigation and analysis of safety incidents and utility performance, and forward-looking risk  

[8:2:66,337] assessment to identify necessary regulatory rule reform. The PUC also performs operation and maintenance audits, outage  

[8:3:66,347] inspections, and investigations of incidents at electric generation facilities.  

[9:0:66,365] The PUC conducts triennial reviews of utility operations to determine just and reasonable rates for energy services. The PUC  

[9:1:66,375] provides guidelines for investor-owned utilities—and to a lesser extent, as directed by statute, an increasing number of  

[9:2:66,385] Community Choice Aggregators, Energy Service Providers, and Core Transport Agents—to follow when purchasing electricity  

[9:3:66,396] and natural gas on behalf of their customers to ensure that sufficient amounts of electricity are procured when and where  

[9:4:66,406] needed in the state.  

[10:0:66,424] Environmental protection and the impact of climate change are critical factors in PUC regulation. The PUC is charged with  

[10:1:66,434] implementing and overseeing key components of Chapter 547, Statutes of 2015 (SB 350), Chapter 312, Statutes of 2018 (SB  

[10:2:66,444] 100), and other climate change legislation, including promoting ratepayer-funded energy efficiency programs, Integrated  

[10:3:66,454] Resource Planning, the state's mandated renewable energy programs, programs for disadvantaged communities, electric  

[10:4:66,464] vehicle programs, and other clean energy programs.  

[11:0:66,482] The PUC oversees programs and regulations to promote both natural gas and electricity reliability and resilience.  

[12:0:66,500] The PUC establishes requirements for energy programs for low-income ratepayers, including programs providing rate  

[12:1:66,510] discounts, financial assistance with energy bills, and the Energy Savings Assistance Program, which provides no-cost  

[12:2:66,520] weatherization services to customers who meet low-income eligibility criteria.  

[13:0:66,538] The PUC conducts and manages environmental reviews pursuant to the California Environmental Quality Act for transmission,  

[13:1:66,548] telecommunications, and other infrastructure projects.  

[14:0:66,566] The PUC develops and implements policies for the rapidly changing communications and broadband markets, including  

[14:1:66,576] monitoring competitive markets for proper operation; enforcing carrier service standards for telecommunication services;  

[14:2:66,586] regulating rural telecommunication rates; protecting consumers against carrier abuses and fraud by applying licensing  

[14:3:66,596] requirements and rules for offering and terminating telecommunication services; and managing efficient deployment of area  

[14:4:66,607] codes and telephone numbers. The PUC is responsible for ensuring that California's investor-owned water utilities deliver  

[14:5:66,617] clean, safe, and reliable water to their customers at reasonable rates. The PUC's water utility work includes investigating water  

[14:6:66,627] and sewer service quality, promoting water conservation and metering, improving low-income programs, analyzing and  

[14:7:66,637] processing rate change requests, and tracking and certifying compliance with PUC requirements.  

[15:0:66,655] The Public Advisor's Office provides procedural information, advice, and assistance to individuals and groups interested in  

[15:1:66,665] participating in PUC proceedings and advises the Commission on procedural matters related to public participation in  

[15:2:66,675] proceedings. The Consumer Affairs Branch (CAB) responds to questions regarding utility service and bills, and assists  

[15:3:66,685] consumers who have unresolved disputes with regulated utilities through an informal process. CAB also provides data on the  

[15:4:66,696] consumer protection issues it handles to stakeholders and decision makers to inform policy making. A Supplier Diversity  

[15:5:66,706] program promotes and monitors supplier diversity in procurement by utilities and oversees a certification clearinghouse.  

[16:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 49 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,13] GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[1:0:416,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — GG   47 

[2:0:173,32] 8660   Public Utilities Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] 6685 - UNIVERSAL SERVICE TELECOMMUNICATIONS PROGRAMS 

[4:0:66,86] The PUC oversees telecommunication-based universal service programs (California Lifeline Fund, California Teleconnect  

[4:1:66,97] Fund, Deaf and Disabled Telecommunications Program, California Advanced Service Fund, and two California High Cost  

[4:2:66,107] Funds), all of which provide needed services to consumers and communities. The objectives of these "universal service"  

[4:3:66,117] programs include: (1) ensuring basic telephone service is available to all Californians; (2) improving access to broadband  

[4:4:66,127] technology to bridge the digital divide; and (3) ensuring consumers have access to sufficient information to make informed  

[4:5:66,137] choices about basic service and universal lifeline telephone services. 

[5:0:66,155] The California LifeLine program provides discounted basic residential wireless and wireline telephone service to qualified low  

[5:1:66,165] income households. The California Teleconnect Fund program provides discounted telecommunication services to qualifying  

[5:2:66,175] schools, libraries, hospitals, and community-based organizations. The Deaf and Disabled Telecommunications Program mainly  

[5:3:66,186] provides equipment and telephone access services to people with hearing disabilities. The California Advanced Services Fund  

[5:4:66,196] Program mainly encourages the deployment of broadband infrastructure in unserved and underserved rural and urban areas of  

[5:5:66,206] California. The California High Cost Fund programs provide subsidies to carriers obligated to provide telephone service to  

[5:6:66,216] customers residing in rural and high-cost areas.  

[6:0:66,234] 6690 - REGULATION OF TRANSPORTATION 

[7:0:66,252] The PUC oversees the safety of railroads, rail transit systems, rail crossings, and other forms of motor conveyance in  

[7:1:66,262] California. All railroads, 7 major rail transit agencies, 6 smaller transit systems, and more than 13,500 public and private  

[7:2:66,272] crossings are subject to PUC regulation and inspection. For traditional heavy rail, the PUC's specially trained and federally-

[7:3:66,282] certified inspectors inspect all tracks (annually), all train equipment and facilities (thrice annually), and all signals and  

[7:4:66,292] appurtenances; investigate all rail accidents resulting in loss of life and property damage; and ensure safe transport of  

[7:5:66,303] hazardous materials by rail.  

[8:0:66,320] For rail transit systems, PUC inspectors and engineers conduct safety inspections of track, vehicles, signals and train control,  

[8:1:66,331] and operations, as well as overall agency triennial safety audits. The PUC also has exclusive authority to approve or  

[8:2:66,341] disapprove all highway-rail crossings of railroad or rail transit tracks, as well as crossing safety treatments deployed at each  

[8:3:66,351] crossing. Additionally, the PUC jointly (with Caltrans) administers the state's annual allocation of federal funds for crossing  

[8:4:66,361] hazard elimination projects and the state's grade separation program, which assists localities with the high cost of grade-

[8:5:66,371] separating highway/rail crossings.  

[9:0:66,389] The transportation program also oversees passenger carriers, including privately-owned for-hire passenger transportation  

[9:1:66,399] companies (e.g., limousines, airport shuttles, transportation network companies, and charter and scheduled bus operators) and  

[9:2:66,409] privately-owned water ferries. The program regulates rates and services, issues certificates and other licenses, enforces safety  

[9:3:66,420] standards and insurance requirements, and protects consumers from for-hire passenger carriers operating illegally.  

[10:0:66,437] 6695 - PUBLIC ADVOCATE'S OFFICE  

[11:0:66,455] The mandate and function of the Public Advocate's Office is defined in Public Utilities Code Section 309.5. The Public  

[11:1:66,465] Advocate's Office is a statutorily-defined state entity charged with representing and advocating on behalf of public utility  

[11:2:66,475] customers and subscribers in all significant proceedings within the PUC's jurisdiction.  

[12:0:66,493] Chapter 856, Statutes of 1996 (SB 960), codified the Public Advocate's Office with a director appointed by and serving at the  

[12:1:66,503] pleasure of the Governor and charged the Public Advocate's Office with representing ratepayer interests in PUC proceedings.  

[12:2:66,514] Chapter 1005, Statutes of 1999 (AB 1658), directed funds from the Public Utilities Commission Utilities Reimbursement  

[12:3:66,524] Account to be transferred in the annual budget act to the Public Utilities Commission Public Advocate's Office Account. The law  

[12:4:66,534] further required that "funds in the Public Utilities Commission Public Advocate's Office Account shall be utilized exclusively by  

[12:5:66,544] the division in the performance of its duties as determined by the director" of the Public Advocates Office.  

[13:0:66,562] Chapter 440, Statutes of 2001 (SB 201), removed the limitation that the Public Advocate's Office only represent ratepayers in  

[13:1:66,572] PUC proceedings. SB 201 extended the Public Advocate's Office representation to other venues such as, but not limited to, the  

[13:2:66,582] Energy Commission, Air Resources Board, California Independent System Operator, and legislative hearings.

[1:0:77,43] 8780   Milton Marks "Little Hoover" Commission on California State Government  

[1:1:228,57] Organization and Economy  

[1:2:66,78] The Milton Marks "Little Hoover" Commission on California State Government Organization and Economy (Commission) is the  

[1:3:66,89] state's only independent and citizen-based oversight mechanism. It conducts four to five comprehensive reviews of executive  

[1:4:66,99] branch programs, departments, and agencies each year and recommends ways to improve performance by increasing  

[1:5:66,109] efficiency and effectiveness with existing resources. The Commission is statutorily responsible for analyzing and making  

[1:6:66,119] recommendations to the Legislature on all Governor reorganization plans.  

[2:0:66,140] The Commission is composed of two members of the Senate, two members of the Assembly, and nine unpaid citizen members  

[2:1:66,150] - five appointed by the Governor and four appointed by the Legislature.  

[3:0:66,170] Because of the Commission's independence, the Legislature charged it with oversight of the California State Auditor, including  

[3:1:66,180] contracting with an independent auditor for annually examining the State Audit Fund.

[1:0:153,43] 8820   Commission on the Status of Women and Girls  

[2:0:66,64] The California Commission on the Status of Women and Girls was established in 1965 to identify and work to eliminate  

[2:1:66,74] inequities in state laws, practices and conditions that affect California's women and girls. The Commission regularly assesses

[1:0:182,43] 8830   California Law Revision Commission  

[2:0:66,64] The California Law Revision Commission (Commission) has the responsibility to make a continuing substantive review of  

[2:1:66,74] California statutory and decisional law, to recommend legislation to make needed reforms, and to make recommendations to  

[2:2:66,84] the Governor and Legislature for revision of the law on major topics (as assigned by the Legislature) that require detailed study  

[2:3:66,95] and cannot easily be handled in the ordinary legislative process.  

[3:0:66,115] The Commission consists of seven gubernatorial appointees plus one Senator, one Assembly Member, and the Legislative  

[3:1:66,125] Counsel. The Commission's work is independent, nonpartisan, and objective.  

[4:0:66,143] The Penal Code is a complex statutory structure that requires study and recommendations for revision. Effective  

[4:1:66,153] January   1,   2020, there will be a new committee within the Commission, which will be responsible for recommending reforms to  

[4:2:66,163] simplify and rationalize criminal law and criminal procedures included in the Penal Code.

[0:0:66,13] GENERAL GOVERNMENT   2019-20 STATE BUDGET — GG   73 

[1:0:197,43] 8855   California State Auditor's Office  

[2:0:66,64] The California State Auditor (State Auditor) promotes the effective and efficient administration and management of public funds  

[2:1:66,74] and programs by providing citizens and government officials nonpartisan, accurate, and objective assessments of state and  

[2:2:66,84] local governments' financial and operational activities. As the independent auditor, the state law exclusively grants the State  

[2:3:66,95] Auditor full access to all records of state and local agencies, special districts, school districts, and any publicly-created entity.  

[2:4:66,105] The State Auditor conducts performance, financial, or compliance audits that are either mandated by statute or requested by  

[2:5:66,115] the Legislature through the Joint Legislative Audit Committee (JLAC). Further, the State Auditor is responsible for annually  

[2:6:66,125] conducting California's statewide Single Audit - a combination of the independent audit of the State's basic financial statements  

[2:7:66,135] and the independent audit of numerous federal programs administered by California. The Single Audit is federally required as a  

[2:8:66,146] condition for California to receive billions in federal funds each year. In 2011-12, the Legislature enacted a program that  

[2:9:66,156] requires the State Auditor to conduct pilot audits of the procurement practices of six trial courts in 2012-13 and thereafter  

[2:10:66,166] biennially audit the procurement practices of the Administrative Office of the Courts and five judicial branch entities - which  

[2:11:66,176] includes trial courts, Appellate Courts, and the Habeas Corpus Resource Center.  

[3:0:66,197] The State Auditor also identifies statewide issues or specific government entities as being at-high risk for waste, fraud, abuse,  

[3:1:66,207] and mismanagement or that have major challenges related to efficiency and effectiveness. The State Auditor has the authority  

[3:2:66,217] to conduct evaluations of those issues and entities identified as being high risk. Similar to the state high-risk program, Chapter  

[3:3:66,227] 451, Statutes of 2011 (AB 187), also authorizes the State Auditor, to the extent resources are available, to identify local  

[3:4:66,237] government agencies - including city, county, or special districts or publicly-created entities - as being at-high risk for the  

[4:0:66,248] potential of waste, fraud, abuse, or mismanagement or that have major challenges associated with their economy, efficiency, or  

[4:1:66,258] effectiveness. Further, if approved by the JLAC, the State Auditor can audit these identified local entities.  

[5:0:66,278] Additionally, under the California Whistleblower Protection Act, the State Auditor's office has broad authority to perform  

[6:0:66,288] independent investigations into allegations of improper governmental activities by employees at state agencies and courts. The  

[6:1:66,299] State Auditor's office administers California's Whistleblower Hotline that enables the public to report allegations of improper  

[6:2:66,309] acts committed by the courts, state agencies, departments, or employees, as defined by law.  

[7:0:66,329] The Voters FIRST Act, approved by voters in November 2008 as Proposition 11, requires the State Auditor to initiate an  

[7:1:66,339] application process for selecting the members of a 14-member Citizens Redistricting Commission (Commission) every 10  

[7:2:66,350] years. The State Auditor develops regulations to implement the application and selection process of the Commission and  

[7:3:66,360] conducts comprehensive outreach to prospective applicants to serve on the Commission.

[1:0:222,43] 8860   Department of Finance  

[1:1:66,64] By statute, the Director of Finance serves as the Governor's chief fiscal policy advisor and promotes long-term economic  

[1:2:66,74] sustainability and responsible resource allocation.  

[1:3:66,95] The primary functions of the Department of Finance are to:  

[1:4:65,112] • Prepare, explain, and administer the annual financial plan for the state.  

[1:5:65,129] • Establish fiscal policies for all state departments.  

[1:6:65,145] • Analyze proposed legislation for fiscal and policy impacts.  

[2:0:65,161] • Monitor and audit expenditures by state departments to ensure compliance with the law, approved standards, and policies.  

[2:1:65,177] • Analyze the fiscal impact of information technology projects.

[6:0:66,311] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:1:66,329] 6770 - STATE BUDGET  

[7:0:66,350] To ensure the financial integrity of the state through the planned allocation of state resources, the Department of Finance  

[7:1:66,360] advises the Governor on the fiscal condition of the state and prepares the annual Governor's Budget. The Governor's Budget  

[7:2:66,370] contains past year actual expenditures and revenues, current year estimated expenditures and revenues, and proposed  

[7:3:66,380] expenditures and estimated revenues for the next fiscal year. After the budget is enacted, the Department of Finance assists in  

[7:4:66,390] the administration of the budget. The Department of Finance also conducts performance reviews of state agencies and  

[7:5:66,401] programs, recommends ways to lower the costs of state government and to better serve the public within existing resources,  

[7:6:66,411] and provides fiscal analyses of bills introduced in the Legislature and makes recommendations to the Governor. Basic and  

[7:7:66,421] applied economic and tax research functions support the revenue estimates required for the development of the state financial  

[7:8:66,431] plan, for the analysis of financial legislation, and the evaluation of economic trends or other fiscal developments which affect  

[7:9:66,441] the state. Basic and applied demographic research functions provide data to support the workload estimates of state agencies.  

[7:10:66,459] 6775 - FINANCIAL INFORMATION SYSTEM FOR CALIFORNIA (FI$Cal) PROJECT SUPPORT  

[8:0:66,477] The Department of Finance is one of four partner agencies responsible for the development, implementation, utilization, and  

[8:1:66,487] maintenance of an integrated statewide financial management system, FI$Cal. This program includes Department of Finance  

[8:2:66,497] subject-matter experts assigned to the FI$Cal Project.  

[8:3:66,515] 6780 - STATE AUDITS AND EVALUATIONS  

[9:0:66,535] The Department of Finance systematically reviews and evaluates state-administered and financed programs. The Office of  

[9:1:66,545] State Audits and Evaluations assists the Director of Finance in fulfilling the statutory responsibilities for supervision over all  

[9:2:66,556] matters concerning the financial and business policies of the state by conducting financial audits and by performing critical  

[9:3:66,566] examinations of state programs and policies including the oversight activities associated with the dissolution of redevelopment  

[9:4:66,576] agencies.  

[10:0:66,594] 6785 - STATEWIDE ACCOUNTING POLICIES, CONSULTING AND TRAINING  

[11:0:66,611] The Statewide Accounting Policies, Consulting and Training Unit sets statewide accounting policies and procedures. The unit  

[11:1:66,622] provides fiscal consulting, training, and support services to departments to help ensure that the state's assets are protected  

[11:2:66,632] and accurate, and timely financial information is maintained. The unit maintains a uniform and complete accounting system for  

[11:3:66,642] state departments and participates in the development of other statewide financial systems.  

[12:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[1:0:164,43] 8880   Financial Information System for California  

[2:0:66,64] The Department of FI$Cal is responsible for supporting the Financial Information System for California (FI$Cal) Project,  

[3:0:66,74] including supporting the system's customers and stakeholders, onboarding any new, deferred, or exempt entities, as well as  

[4:0:66,84] assuming responsibility for system maintenance, upgrades, and enhancements as portions of the system are implemented and  

[4:1:66,95] accepted.  

[5:0:66,115] The FI$Cal Project is a partnership of four control agencies: the Department of Finance, the State Controller's Office, the State  

[5:1:66,125] Treasurer's Office, and the Department of General Services. FI$Cal will provide the state with a single integrated financial  

[6:0:66,135] management system that encompasses budgeting, accounting, procurement, cash management, and financial management  

[7:0:66,146] and reporting. FI$Cal, through the adoption of best business practices, will: reengineer business processes; improve efficiency;  

[7:1:66,156] enhance decision making and resource management; and provide reliable, accessible, and timely statewide financial  

[8:0:66,166] information allowing the state to be more transparent.   FI$Cal further enables transparency through its Open FI$Cal website,  

[8:1:66,176] which was implemented in 2018 pursuant to Government Code section 11862.   Although Open FI$Cal is currently in its pilot  

[9:0:66,186] form, the website will continue to expand providing the public with access to a growing number of the state's non-confidential  

[9:1:66,197] expenditures.

[7:0:66,429] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[7:1:66,447] 6890 - STATEWIDE SYSTEMS DEVELOPMENT  

[8:0:66,468] This program is responsible for the development, implementation, utilization, and maintenance of FI$Cal. FI$Cal will replace  

[8:1:66,478] existing legacy financial systems and significantly improve California's financial management and administration processes.  

[8:2:66,488] This program is comprised of a statewide multi-disciplinary team of staff working collaboratively to implement FI$Cal. The  

[8:3:66,498] statewide project is organized into the following six divisions:  

[8:4:65,516] • Administrative Services Division  

[8:5:65,532] • Business Operation and Solutions Division  

[8:6:65,548] • Communications Division  

[8:7:65,565] • Executive Office  

[8:8:65,581] • Information Technology Division

[1:0:196,43] 8885   Commission on State Mandates  

[2:0:66,64] The Commission on State Mandates timely renders sound, quasi-judicial decisions, in compliance with Article XIII B, Section 6  

[2:1:66,74] of the California Constitution, resolving disputes regarding reimbursement for state-mandated local programs and relieving  

[2:2:66,84] unnecessary congestion of the courts. Its mission is to fairly and impartially hear and determine matters filed by state and local  

[2:3:66,95] government; resolve complex legal questions in a deliberative and timely manner; and produce clear, well-reasoned, and lawful  

[2:4:66,105] decisions.  

[3:0:66,123] The Commission is a quasi-judicial body consisting of the Director of Finance, the State Controller, the State Treasurer, the  

[3:1:66,133] Director of the Office of Planning and Research, a public member with experience in public finance, and two members from the  

[3:2:66,143] categories of city council member, county supervisor, or school district governing board member, appointed by the Governor  

[3:3:66,153] and approved by the Senate.

[3:0:66,69] 6900 - ADMINISTRATION  

[3:1:66,86] The Commission's statutory responsibilities are to hear and decide:  

[3:2:65,104] • Test claims of local agencies and school districts that allege the existence of reimbursable state-mandated programs; and if  

[3:3:76,114] a test claim is approved, adopt parameters and guidelines for claiming reimbursement and prepare a statewide cost  

[3:4:76,124] estimate.  

[4:0:65,141] • Claims alleging that the State Controller's Office has incorrectly reduced payments to local agencies and school districts.  

[4:1:65,157] • Requests for mandate redeterminations that allege the state's liability for a mandate has been modified based on a  

[4:2:76,167] subsequent change in law.  

[4:3:65,183] • Requests to review the claiming instructions issued by the State Controller.  

[4:4:65,199] • Requests to determine whether a mandated program, for which appropriations have been made by the Legislature in any  

[4:5:76,210] three consecutive years, should be included in the State Mandates Apportionment System.  

[4:6:65,226] • Existence of significant financial distress for applicant counties seeking to reduce their General Assistance Aid payments.

[1:0:231,43] 8940   Military Department  

[2:0:66,64] The Military Department is responsible for the command, leadership and management of the California National Guard, Youth  

[2:1:66,74] and Community Programs, State Military Reserve and the Naval Militia. The California Military Department, under proper  

[2:2:66,84] authority, organizes, resources, and trains forces with unique capabilities to serve the community, state, and nation. The  

[2:3:66,95] purpose of the California National Guard is to provide mission ready forces to the federal government as directed by the  

[2:4:66,105] President, emergency public safety support to civil authorities as directed by the Governor, and support to our member's  

[2:5:66,115] families and to the community. With an authorized strength of 22,325, the Army National Guard and Air National Guard are  

[2:6:66,125] organized, manned, and funded in accordance with federal Departments of the Army and Air Force regulatory guidance.  

[3:0:66,146] The Military Department Youth and Community Program serves California communities and families by delivering national  

[3:1:66,156] level, high quality educational support programs, in partnership with the educational community, within a military, academic  

[3:2:66,166] structured environment. In addition to the funding that flows through the State Treasury, the Military Department also receives  

[3:3:66,176] federal funding directly from the Department of Defense. A special display titled "Other Federal Funds" shows the funding  

[3:4:66,186] received from this source.  

[4:0:66,207] Because the Military Department's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay  

[4:1:66,217] program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[4:0:66,94] 6911 - NATIONAL GUARD

[5:1:66,125] State Military Reserve. Army National Guard support plans include a community-based land force, logistics, communications,  

[5:2:66,135] law enforcement and other specialized support. Air National Guard support plans include rescue, air defense, airlift and  

[5:3:66,145] unmanned aerial systems, space, intelligence, communications, and other specialized services. The Office of the Adjutant  

[5:4:66,156] General element governs the joint activities and performance of the Military Department in areas such as personnel and fiscal  

[5:5:66,166] resource management, judicial affairs, internal controls, facility management, and information technology. The Military Civil  

[5:6:66,176] Support element provides liaison and coordination with federal, state, and local agencies so that mutual understanding and  

[5:7:66,186] unity of purpose is assured during an emergency. The objectives of this element are to plan, prepare, and train for the  

[5:8:66,196] deployment of Military Department personnel and equipment to support civil authority when called to state service by the  

[5:9:66,207] Governor due to domestic emergency or natural disaster and to provide the state, county, city, and other public agencies with  

[5:10:66,217] the coordination necessary to ensure a timely, organized response. The State Military Reserve is a volunteer component of the  

[5:11:66,227] Military Department whose mission is to provide a trained, disciplined and ready force during training, preparation for  

[5:12:66,237] mobilization, demobilization, and provision of support to civil authorities during periods of state emergencies. The federal  

[5:13:66,247] government provides virtually all supplies, equipment, transportation, subsistence, and support services necessary for training,  

[5:14:66,258] deployment, and commitment of the National Guard. Training is conducted in accordance with the Department of the Army and  

[5:15:66,268] Air Force Regulations and Training Guidance.  

[6:0:66,286] 6912 - YOUTH AND COMMUNITY PROGRAMS  

[7:0:66,303] The Military Department manages and maintains partnerships with the following nine programs while serving more than 13,000  

[7:1:66,313] youth annually: California Cadet Corps, Oakland Military Institute, California Military Institute, Porterville Military Academy,  

[7:2:66,324] Grizzly Youth Academy, Sunburst Youth Academy, Discovery ChalleNGe Academy, STARBASE Academy Sacramento and  

[7:3:66,334] STARBASE Academy Los Alamitos. The California Cadet Corps, founded in 1911 by the California Legislature and then-

[7:4:66,344] Adjutant General BG Edwin Forbes, is a school-based, applied leadership program that is designed to provide maximum  

[7:5:66,354] growth and leadership opportunities for cadets from elementary through high school levels. It provides leadership opportunities  

[7:6:66,364] for cadets by allowing them to conduct training for junior cadets, perform as leaders in their cadet military units, and by  

[8:0:66,375] demonstrating proper behavior and citizenship at their schools and in their communities. As part of their training, cadets provide  

[8:1:66,385] assistance and support to the school and community. The Oakland Military Institute, California Military Institute, and Porterville  

[8:2:66,395] Military Academy develops leaders of character by providing rigorous programs to promote excellence in academics,  

[9:0:66,405] leadership, citizenship, athletics, and physical fitness/well being. Additionally, the programs instill the 10 Cadet Corps values of  

[9:1:66,415] loyalty, education, ambition, duty, enthusiasm, respect, service, health, integrity, and personal courage. Using a military  

[10:0:66,426] framework, these programs graduate cadets who are capable of meeting the admissions requirements for college and who are  

[10:1:66,436] prepared for their roles as future leaders. The mission of the National Guard Youth ChalleNGe Program is to intervene in and  

[10:2:66,446] reclaim the lives of 16-18 year old high school dropouts. Graduates leave the program with the values, life skills, education, and  

[10:3:66,456] self-discipline necessary to succeed as productive citizens. The goal of the Department of Defense STARBASE program is to  

[10:4:66,466] motivate elementary school students, primarily 5th graders, to explore Science, Technology, Engineering and Math (STEM) as  

[10:5:66,477] they continue their education. The curriculum is designed to increase student involvement and interest in STEM, enhance their  

[10:6:66,487] understanding of the role that STEM literacy plays in their lives, strengthen potential for future careers, and make the pursuit of  

[10:7:66,497] STEM activities more attractive and accessible. While attending STARBASE students interact with military personnel to explore  

[10:8:66,507] careers and observe STEM applications in the "real world." These nine youth programs are financed with federal, state and  

[10:9:66,517] local funds. The California National Guard is involved in youth programs because political and community leaders at the  

[10:10:66,528] federal, state, and local levels recognize that the National Guard brings structure, discipline, and effective leadership training  

[10:11:66,538] methods to the educational setting.

[8:0:66,433] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[9:0:66,452] The Military Department's statewide facilities include 95 active armories, 4 aviation centers, 23 field maintenance shops, 

[9:1:66,462] 2 combined support maintenance shops, and 2 maneuver area training equipment sites. The total real property assets of the  

[9:2:66,472] Military Department encompass an area of 7.8 million square feet. These facilities are used to house and train the California  

[9:3:66,482] National Guard and provide emergency public safety support. The Military Department also operates three major training  

[9:4:66,492] facilities consisting of troop lodging, administration, warehouse, maintenance, firing ranges, and maneuver training areas.

[0:0:66,13] 2019-20 STATE BUDGET — GG   128   GENERAL GOVERNMENT 

[1:0:199,43] 8955   Department of Veterans Affairs  

[2:0:66,64] The California Department of Veterans Affairs (CalVet) honors and serves California's veterans and their families by ensuring  

[2:1:66,74] our veterans have access to the benefits they have earned to enable them to meet their needs. The CalVet promotes and  

[2:2:66,84] delivers innovative services through efficient, cost-effective programs and collaboration with key stakeholders and partners.  

[3:0:66,105] More specifically, the CalVet:  

[3:1:65,123] • Communicates with California's veterans and their families at all pivotal moments throughout their post-military lives to  

[3:2:76,133] educate them and connect them to their federal, state, and local benefits.  

[4:0:65,149] • Provides California veterans and their families with direct representation in presenting their claims and appeals for benefits  

[4:1:76,159] before the US Department of Veterans Affairs and the federal Board of Veteran Appeals.  

[5:0:65,175] • Provides California veterans the opportunity to achieve the American dream of homeownership through direct low-cost loans  

[5:1:76,186] to acquire farms and homes.  

[6:0:65,202] • Provides the state's aged or disabled veterans with premier rehabilitative, residential, and medical care and services in a  

[6:1:76,212] home-like environment at the California Veterans Homes.  

[7:0:66,240] This mission is based upon the philosophy that benefit programs for veterans fulfill necessary, proper, and valid public  

[8:0:66,250] purposes; promote patriotism; recognize and reward sacrifice and service to our country; and provide needed readjustment  

[8:1:66,260] assistance to returning veterans and their families, whose lives were interrupted when they responded to our country's call to  

[8:2:66,270] military service.  

[9:0:66,291] Because the CalVet's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay program to  

[9:1:66,301] support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[13:0:66,350] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES  

[13:1:65,369] • Program Review and Audits - The Budget includes $749,000 General Fund, $83,000 Veterans Farm and Home Building  

[13:2:76,379] Fund of 1943, and 6 positions to establish a Program Review and Internal Audits Unit. This unit will enable CalVet to be  

[13:3:76,389] proactive and regularly review and audit its programs and operations, thereby helping to improve the effectiveness and  

[13:4:76,399] efficiency of operations.  

[14:0:65,415] • California Transition Assistance Program (CalTAP) - The Budget includes an additional $1.1 million General Fund and 8  

[14:1:76,426] positions for CalTAP. CalTAP is a state-wide transition assistance program that provides California veterans education, direct  

[14:2:76,436] services, and referrals to ensure veterans have efficient and effective access to their earned benefits. This funding will  

[14:3:76,446] expand in-person trainings for this population throughout the state.  

[15:0:65,462] • Care Staffing and Operations -   The Budget includes $6,268,000 one-time General Fund in 2019-20 to address maintenance  

[15:1:76,472] and rising operational costs at   three Veterans Homes in California. The Budget also includes $829,000 ongoing General  

[15:2:76,483] Fund to comply with new federal regulations for pharmacy services and compliance oversight.

[5:0:66,315] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[6:0:66,334] 6990 - FARM AND HOME LOANS TO VETERANS  

[7:0:66,354] The CalVet Home Loan Program offers veterans, meeting specified requirements, loans to purchase, construct, or rehabilitate  

[7:1:66,364] new or existing single family dwellings, which include condominiums, units in shared equity cooperative housing developments,  

[7:2:66,374] mobile homes, and farms. The CalVet also offers Home Improvement Loans to active CalVet customers or homeowners who  

[7:3:66,385] own their homes free of debt.  

[7:4:66,402] 6995 - VETERANS CLAIMS AND RIGHTS  

[8:0:66,423] The Veteran Services Division provides service and assistance to California's veterans, dependents, and survivors. Programs  

[8:1:66,433] administered consist of: Veterans Dependents Educational Assistance Program, County Veterans Service Office Program,  

[8:2:66,443] Veterans Outreach Program, California Transition Assistance Program, Veterans Housing and Homelessness Prevention  

[8:3:66,453] program, Medi-Cal Cost Avoidance Program, Veterans Claims and Appeals Representation, State Veteran Cemeteries,  

[8:4:66,463] California State Approving Agency for Veterans Education, Disabled Veteran Business Enterprise Program, Veterans Mental  

[8:5:66,474] Health Program including Veteran Justice Outreach and Incarcerated Veterans, Homeless Veterans Outreach Program,  

[8:6:66,484] Veterans Driver License Program, and the Veterans License Plate Program. California, with approximately 1.6 million veterans,  

[8:7:66,494] represents 8.2 percent of the nation's total veteran population.  

[8:8:66,512] 7000 - CARE OF SICK AND DISABLED VETERANS  

[9:0:66,532] CalVet operates eight licensed long-term care facilities which provide affordable, quality services to eligible veterans and  

[9:1:66,542] spouses. The Veterans Homes also provide therapeutic care and rehabilitative services to help   sustain activities of daily living  

[9:2:66,553] and independent function. The primary services provided by these Veterans Homes are:  

[9:3:65,570] • The Veterans Home of California, Barstow offers skilled nursing, intermediate, and domiciliary care.  

[9:4:65,586] • The Veterans Home of California, Chula Vista offers skilled nursing, assisted living, and domiciliary care.  

[9:5:65,603] • The Veterans Home of California, Fresno offers skilled nursing (including memory care) and assisted living care.  

[9:6:65,619] • The Veterans Home of California, Greater Los Angeles and Ventura County (GLAVC), includes three main campuses: West  

[9:7:76,629] Los Angeles (WLA), Lancaster, and Ventura. All three offer assisted living care. The WLA campus also offers skilled nursing  

[9:8:76,639] care (including memory care).  

[10:0:65,655] • The Veterans Home of California, Redding offers skilled nursing (including memory care) and assisted living care.  

[10:1:65,672] • The Veterans Home of California, Yountville is the largest veterans home in the United States. It offers all four levels of care:  

[10:2:76,682] skilled nursing (including memory care), intermediate, assisted living, and domiciliary care.  

[10:3:66,700] 7005 - VETERANS MEMORIALS

[5:0:66,471] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW  

[6:0:66,489] The Department of Veterans Affairs (CalVet) facilities include eight veterans homes, three state veterans cemeteries, and a  

[6:1:66,500] headquarters office building. The eight veterans homes are located in Yountville, Barstow, Chula Vista, West Los Angeles,  

[7:0:66,510] Lancaster, Ventura, Redding, and Fresno on 766 acres of land, with 2.4 million gross square feet of building space. Veterans  

[8:0:66,520] homes provide domiciliary housing for the state's veterans and are also licensed to provide three levels of care, ranging from  

[8:1:66,530] assisted living to skilled nursing. The three cemeteries operated by CalVet are located in Igo, near Redding, Yountville, and  

[9:0:66,540] Seaside, and contain a total of approximately 24,000 gravesites on 91 usable acres.   A pre-application has been submitted to  

[9:1:66,551] the United States Department of Veterans Affairs for a fourth state cemetery to be located in Southern California.

[1:0:259,43] 9100   Tax Relief  

[2:0:66,64] This budget provides funding for cities and counties to help defray the loss of revenue as a result of the Homeowners' Property  

[2:1:66,74] Tax Relief Program, that provides California homeowners assistance through a $7,000 exemption from property tax, and the  

[2:2:66,84] Open Space Subventions Program, that provides tax relief to individuals who agreed to hold their land as open space under the  

[2:3:66,95] Williamson Act of 1965. In addition, this budget receives the excess funds from loan repayments from individuals who  

[2:4:66,105] participated in the Senior Citizens' Property Tax Postponement Program, provides funding to help cities and counties  

[2:5:66,115] accurately report population data for the 2020 United States Census, and helps defray the local agency costs of recall elections  

[2:6:66,125] in 2018.

[3:0:66,88] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[3:1:66,106] 7500 - HOMEOWNERS' PROPERTY TAX RELIEF  

[4:0:66,127] In order to reduce California taxpayers' property tax burden, the California Constitution exempts homeowners from paying  

[4:1:66,137] property taxes on the first $7,000 of the assessed value of their principal place of residence. The Constitution requires the state  

[4:2:66,147] to reimburse local governments for revenues lost due to the homeowners' exemption.  

[4:3:66,165] 7505 - SUBVENTIONS FOR OPEN SPACE  

[5:0:66,182] The Williamson Act permits cities and counties to enter into contracts with landowners to limit the use of land to agricultural,  

[5:1:66,193] scenic, and open space purposes. Article XIII, Section 8, of the California Constitution permits land under such restrictions to  

[5:2:66,203] be assessed at other than market value based on its limited uses. The state provides reimbursements to cities and counties to  

[5:3:66,213] partially defray the loss of property tax revenues. The Department of Conservation administers this program.  

[5:4:66,231] 7510 - SENIOR CITIZENS' PROPERTY TAX POSTPONEMENT LOAN REPAYMENTS  

[5:5:66,248] The Senior Citizens' Property Tax Postponement Program allows eligible homeowners to defer payment of residential property  

[5:6:66,259] tax. The state pays the deferred taxes to local governments on behalf of the participants, and places a lien on their property to  

[5:7:66,269] assure repayment when the property is sold or transferred. The Senior Citizens' Property Tax Postponement Loan Repayments  

[5:8:66,279] Program captures repayments from individuals that participated in the Senior Citizens' Property Tax Postponement Program  

[5:9:66,289] and transfers to the General Fund the amount in excess of what is required to fund the Postponement Program claims. The  

[5:10:66,299] Program was suspended in 2009 and was reestablished via legislation in 2014 (Chapter 703, Statutes of 2014).  

[5:11:66,317] 7515 - RECALL ELECTIONS  

[6:0:66,335] Recall Elections provides funding for counties to pay for the costs of state recall elections in 2018, including expenses for  

[6:1:66,345] verifying signatures, printing ballots and voter information guides, and operating polling places in accordance with section  

[6:2:66,355] 11108 of the Elections Code.  

[6:3:66,373] 7520 - LOCAL UPDATE OF CENSUS ADDRESS PROGRAM  

[7:0:66,391] The Local Update of Census Address Program provides population-based incentive grants of between $7,500 and $125,000 to  

[7:1:66,401] cities and counties, to ensure they participate in the review and update of the United States Census Bureau's Master Address  

[7:2:66,411] List for the 2020 Census.  

[8:0:66,452] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM

[1:0:204,43] 9210   Local Government Financing  

[2:0:66,64] The state provides general-purpose revenue to counties, cities, and special districts when special circumstances occur. The

[1:0:171,43] 9620   Cash Management and Budgetary Loans  

[2:0:66,64] The Cash Management and Budgetary Loans budget tracks General Fund expenditures for interest costs related to cash flow  

[2:1:66,74] and budgetary borrowing. Internal and external cash flow borrowing can be used to overcome normal cash flow imbalances  

[2:2:66,84] during the fiscal year. Because receipts and disbursements occur unevenly throughout the fiscal year, the General Fund may  

[2:3:66,95] need to borrow even though its budget is balanced. Budgetary loans may be used to loan monies from various state funds to  

[2:4:66,105] the General Fund to help bridge a budget shortfall. These loans are temporary, and do not harm the operations of departmental  

[2:5:66,115] programs.  

[3:0:66,145] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[11:0:66,545] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS  

[11:1:66,563] 7720 - CASH MANAGEMENT  

[12:0:66,584] Borrowing From Other Funds or Other State/Local Entities  

[13:0:66,604] Chapter 312, Statutes of 1907, authorized the transfers to the General Fund from certain specified funds (often referred to as  

[13:1:66,614] "borrowable" funds) in the State Treasury System whenever the General Fund was exhausted. Those provisions, in slightly  

[13:2:66,624] modified form, are now contained in Sections 16310 and 16418 of the Government Code. This authorization enables the  

[13:3:66,635] General Fund to overcome normal cash flow imbalances throughout the fiscal year to a large extent. The use of these funds'  

[13:4:66,645] money for General Fund cash flow purposes is largely transparent to most of these funds. This mechanism does not interfere  

[13:5:66,655] with the day-to-day cash needs of the borrowable funds because of the implementation method. Generally, when a fund is  

[13:6:66,665] designated as borrowable for General Fund cash flow purposes, it merely increases the amount of cash the State Controller's  

[13:7:66,675] Office is authorized to borrow from the Pooled Money Investment Account (PMIA) when there is a General Fund cash  

[13:8:66,686] shortage. For most of these funds, actual cash is not transferred out of the funds into the General Fund. The entire cash  

[13:9:66,696] balances of these funds remain intact and completely available for the funds' purposes.  

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:136,32] 9620   Cash Management and Budgetary Loans - Continued 

[3:0:66,69] A State Agency Investment Fund (SAIF) Program was enacted pursuant to Chapter 142, Statutes of 2011 (Senate Bill 79,  

[3:1:66,79] Government Code section 16330), to increase internal borrowable resources through the receipt of deposits from a state entity  

[3:2:66,89] that is not required to deposit or invest funds in the PMIA. The minimum deposit or investment per participant is $500 million  

[3:3:66,99] and the maximum amount for the SAIF Program for all participants is $10 billion. There are currently no deposits in this  

[3:4:66,109] Program.  

[4:0:66,130] A Voluntary Investment Program (VIP) was enacted pursuant to Chapter 44, Statutes of 2012 (Senate Bill 1033, Government  

[4:1:66,140] Code section 16340), for the receipt of voluntary deposits from local entities for the purpose of providing additional liquidity for  

[4:2:66,150] the state's cash management. The minimum deposit from a local entity is $200 million and the maximum amount for the VIP  

[4:3:66,160] from all local entities combined is $10 billion. No deposits have been made into this Program.  

[5:0:66,191] Borrowing From Financial Markets  

[6:0:66,211] In addition, the courts have validated temporary external borrowing that meets the "appropriation doctrine." Under this doctrine,  

[6:1:66,222] an obligation is not considered a debt or liability within the State Constitutional limitation on indebtedness (Section 1 of Article  

[6:2:66,232] XVI) if an appropriation is made from existing funds or reasonably anticipated funds subject to appropriation. This doctrine was  

[6:3:66,242] invoked in 1933 and 1936 to uphold the use of registered warrants (IOUs) during the Great Depression and again in 1971 to  

[6:4:66,252] validate the State of California Notes provisions of Chapter 223, Statutes of 1971. The Notes provisions were invoked in  

[6:5:66,262] 1971-72 in lieu of the more costly registered warrant authority used during the Depression and, like registered warrants,  

[6:6:66,273] required the projected exhaustion of all internal sources of funds before being implemented. The Notes provisions were re-

[6:7:66,283] enacted by Chapter 10X, Statutes of 1983, and employed in 1982-83 and 1983-84.  

[7:0:66,303] In 1984-85, the state implemented a cash management program pursuant to Chapter 268, Statutes of 1984, which provided the  

[7:1:66,313] flexibility to borrow from external sources prior to exhausting internal sources. Under this program, the use of external funds  

[7:2:66,324] results in potential savings to the General Fund, as well as increased revenue to the various special funds from which the  

[7:3:66,334] General Fund would have borrowed from pursuant to Sections 16310 and 16418 of the Government Code.  

[8:0:66,354] External sources of borrowing available to the state include revenue anticipation notes (RANs), revenue anticipation warrants  

[8:1:66,364] (RAWs), and registered warrants (short-term IOUs). RANs and RAWs are short-term debt obligations issued in anticipation of  

[8:2:66,375] receiving revenues in the near future.  

[9:0:66,395] Additional costs to the cash management program include, but are not limited to, costs to issue and redeem IOUs, and any  

[9:1:66,405] other program measures necessary to effectively manage cash flow.  

[10:0:66,436] Overall Program  

[11:0:66,456] Pursuant to Government Code Sections 12020 and 12021, accounts payable and receivable and cash flow statements for the  

[11:1:66,466] past, current, and budget years are included as Schedule 5 in the Governor's Budget Summary. Neither cash receipts nor  

[11:2:66,477] disbursements match revenues and expenditures presented elsewhere in this Budget. To reflect cash flow, budget data is  

[11:3:66,487] converted to cash basis primarily by adjusting for cash collected by an agency but not yet recorded by the Controller and for  

[11:4:66,497] accruals. Adjustments are also made to reflect statutory changes, anticipated legislative actions, and administrative actions.  

[12:0:66,517] The estimated current and budget year cash flows incorporate the latest revenue and expenditure budget measures and  

[12:1:66,528] assumptions. During difficult cash periods, cash management measures such as intra-year payment deferrals may be used  

[12:2:66,538] from time-to-time to address low points during the fiscal year.  

[13:0:66,556] 7725 - BUDGETARY LOANS  

[14:0:66,573] The Budget Act authorizes budgetary loans from various funds and accounts to the General Fund. These loans are repaid over  

[14:1:66,583] multiple fiscal years. Unless otherwise specified in statute, the loans are repaid with interest calculated at the Pooled Money  

[14:2:66,594] Investment Account rate on the date of transfer.

[1:0:157,43] 9625   Interest Payments to the Federal Government  

[2:0:66,64] Pursuant to the Cash Management Improvement Act of 1990 (CMIA), the state will incur an interest liability to the federal  

[2:1:66,74] government. The purpose of CMIA is to ensure greater efficiency, effectiveness, and equity in the exchange of funds between  

[2:2:66,84] the federal government and the state. The major provisions of CMIA are: (a) federal agencies must make timely fund  

[2:3:66,95] disbursements and grant awards to the state; (b) the state must minimize the time between the deposit of federal funds in the  

[2:4:66,105] state account and the payout of the funds for program purposes; (c) the state is entitled to interest from the federal government  

[2:5:66,115] from the time state funds are paid out for program purposes until federal funds are deposited in the state account; and (d) the  

[2:6:66,125] federal government is entitled to interest from the state from the time federal funds are deposited in the state account until the  

[2:7:66,135] funds are paid out for program purposes.  

[3:0:66,156] The federal assistance programs impacted by the CMIA are those programs that have $295 million or more in federal fund  

[3:1:66,166] expenditures. For the majority of these programs, state departments request federal funds in advance of the warrant (i.e.,  

[3:2:66,176] check) issuance. State departments use this funding technique because the State Constitution requires that the funds be  

[3:3:66,186] deposited before the warrants are issued.  

[4:0:66,207] Interest payments to the federal government are due no later than March 31 each year. The payment will be for the interest  

[4:1:66,217] liability incurred during the state's prior fiscal year.  

[5:0:66,247] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS

[1:0:168,43] 9650   Health and Dental Benefits for Annuitants  

[2:0:66,64] This program provides funding for health and dental benefit services for retired state employees and their dependents.  

[3:0:66,84] The program began on January 1, 1962, with an employer contribution of $5 per month toward the cost of a basic health plan.  

[3:1:66,95] Since then, major medical plans, Medicare, and plans supplementing Medicare have been developed. Dental care was added  

[3:2:66,105] in 1982.  

[4:0:66,125] The 2018-19 employer contribution for health premiums for employees hired by the state on and prior to December 31, 2016,  

[4:1:66,135] maintains the average 100/90 percent contribution formula established in Government Code Section 22871 for fully vested  

[4:2:66,146] members. Under this formula, the state averages the premiums of the four health benefit plans with the largest state enrollment  

[4:3:66,156] in order to calculate the maximum amount the state contributes towards retiree health benefits. The state also contributes 90  

[4:4:66,166] percent of this average towards the health benefit costs of each of the retiree's dependents. Vesting schedules and employer  

[4:5:66,176] contributions may vary by employee bargaining unit contract.  

[5:0:66,197] The 2018-19 employer contribution for health premiums for employees hired by the state on and after January 1, 2017,  

[5:1:66,207] maintains the average 80/80 percent contribution formula established in Government Code Section 22871.3 for fully vested  

[5:2:66,217] members. Under this formula, the state averages the premiums of the four health benefit plans with the largest state enrollment  

[5:3:66,227] in order to calculate the maximum amount the state contributes towards retiree health benefits. The state also contributes 80  

[5:4:66,237] percent of this average towards the health benefit costs of each of the retiree's dependents. Vesting schedules and employer  

[5:5:66,248] contributions may vary by employee bargaining unit contract.  

[6:0:66,268] The retiree is responsible for paying all health benefit plan costs that exceed the state contribution. Premiums are determined  

[6:1:66,278] on a calendar-year basis; therefore, the state contribution will vary during the fiscal year. The 2018 monthly contribution  

[6:2:66,288] maximums are $725 for a single enrollee, $1,377 for an enrollee and one dependent, and $1,766 for an enrollee and two or  

[6:3:66,299] more dependents. The 2019 monthly contribution maximums are $734 for a single enrollee, $1,398 for an enrollee and one  

[6:4:66,309] dependent, and $1,788 for an enrollee and two or more dependents. Dental care premiums vary by plan and number of  

[6:5:66,319] dependents.  

[7:0:66,339] Effective July 1, 2012, funding for health benefits for California State University annuitants is displayed in Organization Code  

[7:1:66,350] 6645 under Higher Education. This funding was previously budgeted within Organization Code 9650.
